0.4em;text-align:center"><div style="text-align:left">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_women_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of women in the United Kingdom">History of women</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Military history of the United Kingdom">Military history</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Brexit</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>), short <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of <b>British exit from the European Union</b>, is the impending <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union" title="Withdrawal from the European Union">withdrawal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (UK) from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU). In a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">referendum on 23 June 2016</a>, a narrow majority (52%) of those voting supported leaving the EU. On 29 March 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">UK government</a> invoked <a href="/wiki/Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union</a>. The United Kingdom is due to leave the EU on 29 March 2019 at 11 p.m. UK time<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  (midnight <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Time" title="Central European Time">Central European Time</a>), when the period for negotiating a <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union#Procedure" title="Withdrawal from the European Union">withdrawal agreement</a> will end unless an extension is agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> announced the government\'s intention not to seek permanent membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_single_market" class="mw-redirect" title="European single market">European single market</a> or the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Customs_Union" title="European Union Customs Union">EU customs union</a> after leaving the EU<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wilkinson_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and promised to <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union (Withdrawal) Bill">repeal</a> the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities_Act_1972_(UK)" title="European Communities Act 1972 (UK)">European Communities Act of 1972</a> and incorporate existing <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a> into <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law of the United Kingdom">UK domestic law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-trigger_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-trigger-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A new government department, the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" title="Department for Exiting the European Union">Department for Exiting the European Union</a>, was created in July 2016. In 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">British Cabinet</a> agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Chequers_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Chequers Agreement">Chequers Agreement</a>, and these are the current proposals suggested from the UK under the <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Theresa_May" title="Premiership of Theresa May">Premiership of Theresa May</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brexit_negotiations" title="Brexit negotiations">Negotiations with the EU</a> officially started in June 2017, aiming to complete the withdrawal agreement by October 2018. In June 2018, the UK and the EU published a joint progress report outlining agreement on issues including <a href="/wiki/Customs" title="Customs">customs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Value-added_tax" title="Value-added tax">VAT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euratom_Treaty" title="Euratom Treaty">Euratom</a>.\n</p><p>The UK joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities" title="European Communities">European Communities</a> (EC) in 1973, with membership confirmed by a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">referendum in 1975</a>. In the 1970s and 1980s, withdrawal from the EC was advocated mainly by <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> members and <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> figures. From the 1990s, the main advocates of withdrawal were the newly founded <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> (UKIP) and an increasing number of Eurosceptic <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> members. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> held the referendum in fulfilment of a 2015 manifesto pledge. Cameron, who had campaigned for "Remain", resigned after the referendum result and was succeeded by Theresa May, who called a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">snap general election</a> less than a year later, in which she lost her overall majority. Her minority government is <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">supported in key votes</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a>.\n</p><p>Six weeks after the referendum, the Bank of England introduced <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_easing" title="Quantitative easing">quantitative easing</a> and lower interest rates, thus allowing both <a href="/wiki/Depreciation" title="Depreciation">depreciation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">sterling</a> and a rise in inflation that outpaced wage growth for most of 2017. The drop in the value of sterling has been claimed to have been caused in part by hedge-fund managers betting on Brexit against polls predicting a narrow victory for the "Remain" camp.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a broad consensus in existing economic research that Brexit is likely to reduce the UK\'s real <a href="/wiki/Per_capita_income" title="Per capita income">per capita income</a> in the medium term and long term.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> There is also agreement among economists that the Brexit referendum itself damaged the economy in the subsequent two years.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Studies on effects that have materialised since the referendum show annual losses of \xc2\xa3404 for the average UK household from increased inflation, and losses between 2% and 2.5% of UK GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:12_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:20_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Brexit is likely to reduce immigration from <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> (EEA) countries to the UK,<sup id="cite_ref-:18_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and poses challenges for UK higher education and academic research.<sup id="cite_ref-:19_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As of  August 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Brexit&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the size of the "<a href="#Divorce_bill">divorce bill</a>", the UK\'s inheritance of existing EU trade agreements, and <a href="/wiki/Ireland%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="Ireland\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom relations">relations with Ireland</a> and other EU member states remain uncertain. The precise impact on the UK depends on whether the process will be a <a href="#Hard_and_soft_Brexit">"hard" or a "soft" Brexit</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Terminology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span to February 2017 UK white paper</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-55"><a href="#Public_comment_pre-_and_post-Article_50_notification"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="tocnumber">12.4</span> <span class="toctext">US position</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-62"><a href="#Cultural_responses_to_the_referendum_vote"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Cultural responses to the referendum vote</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-63"><a href="#Brexit_in_the_visual_arts"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Brexit in the visual arts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-64"><a href="#Brexit_in_novels"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Brexit in novels</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-65"><a href="#Brexit_in_theatre"><span class="tocnumber">13.3</span> <span class="toctext">Brexit in class="toclevel-1 tocsection-67"><a href="#Establishment_of_pro-European_political_organisations"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Establishment of pro-European political organisations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-68"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-71"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-73"><a href="#Relating_to_court_cases"><span class="tocnumber">19.1</span> <span class="toctext">Relating to court cases</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>\n<p>In the wake of the referendum, many new pieces of Brexit-related jargon have entered popular use.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Brexit</dt>\n<dd><i>Brexit</i> (like its early variant, <i>Brixit</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of "British" and "exit". In popular usage, it was derived by analogy from <a href="/wiki/Greek_withdrawal_from_the_eurozone" title="Greek withdrawal from the eurozone">Grexit</a>, referring to a hypothetical withdrawal of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a> (and possibly also the EU).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brexit,_n_2017_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brexit,_n_2017-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, the term <i>Brexit</i> may have first been used in reference to a possible UK withdrawal from the EU by Peter Wilding, in a <a href="/wiki/Euractiv" class="mw-redirect" title="Euractiv">Euractiv</a> blog post on 15 May 2012 (this is given as the first attestation in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYD_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYD-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brexit,_n_2017_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brexit,_n_2017-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Hard_and_soft_Brexit">Hard and soft Brexit</span></dt>\n<dd>The terms "hard Brexit" and "soft Brexit" are often used unofficially in terms of the process, but commonly by news media,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and are understood to describe the prospective relationship between the UK and the EU after withdrawal, ranging from <i>hard</i>, which could involve the UK trading with the EU under the World Trade Organization\'s <a href="/wiki/Bali_Package" title="Bali Package">Bali Package</a>, but with no obligation to accept <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market#Free_movement_of_persons" title="European Single Market">free movement of people</a>, to <i>soft</i>, which might involve retaining membership of the EU single market <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market#Free_movement_of_goods" title="European Single Market">for goods</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market#Freedom_to_establish_and_provide_services" title="European Single Market">services</a> and at least some free movement of people, according to <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> rules.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The current prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, has prepared to implement the "<a href="/wiki/Chequers_plan" title="Chequers plan">Chequers Plan</a>," which uses some "soft" aspects of Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Blind_Brexit">Blind Brexit</span></dt>\n<dd>Coined in September 2018 to describe a scenario where the UK leaves the EU without clarity on the terms of a future trade deal,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> EU and British negotiators would then have until 31 December 2020 to sign off on a future trade deal, during which time the UK will effectively remain a member of the EU, but with no voting rights.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Divorce_bill">Divorce bill</span></dt>\n<dd>It is expected that the UK will make a contribution toward financial commitments that were approved while the UK was still a member of the EU but are still outstanding. In the first phase of negotiations the total amount was referred to as the <i>single financial settlement</i>, or just <i>the settlement</i>. Especially in the media, this has been called an <i>exit bill</i> or <i>divorce bill</i>, while the EU talk of <i>settling the accounts</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Commons_Briefing_8039_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commons_Briefing_8039-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Brexit Secretary <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Raab" title="Dominic Raab">Dominic Raab</a> has said the UK will not pay its financial settlement to the EU in a no-deal scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Chequers_plan">Chequers plan</span></dt>\n<dd>The short name given by the media to "<i><a href="/wiki/The_future_relationship_between_the_United_Kingdom_and_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="The future relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union">The future relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union</a></i>", the government\'s <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a> drawn up at <a href="/wiki/Chequers" title="Chequers">Chequers</a> and published on 12 July 2018 which sets out the sort of relationship the UK government wants with the EU after Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Brexiteer">Brexiteer</span></dt>\n<dd>Those supporting Brexit are sometimes referred to as "Brexiteers".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt><span id="Remainer">Remainer</span></dt>\n<dd>Those in favour of the UK remaining in the EU are sometimes referred to as "Remainers".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The derogatory term "Remoaner" (a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of "remainer" and "moaner") is sometimes used by pro-Brexit media outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_European_Union%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="History of European Union\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom relations">History of European Union\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Foreign relations of the United Kingdom">Foreign relations of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The "<a href="/wiki/Inner_Six" title="Inner Six">Inner Six</a>" European countries signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1951)" title="Treaty of Paris (1951)">Treaty of Paris</a> in 1951, establishing the <a href="/wiki/European_Coal_and_Steel_Community" title="European Coal and Steel Community">European Coal and Steel Community</a> (ECSC). The 1955 <a href="/wiki/Messina_Conference" title="Messina Conference">Messina Conference</a> deemed that the ECSC was a success, and resolved to extend the concept further, thereby leading to the 1957 <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaties of Rome">Treaties of Rome</a> establishing the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Atomic_Energy_Community" title="European Atomic Energy Community">European Atomic Energy Community</a> (Euratom). In <a href="/wiki/Merger_Treaty" title="Merger Treaty">1967</a> these became known as the European Communities (EC). The UK attempted to join in 1963 and 1967, but these applications were vetoed by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After de Gaulle relinquished the French presidency the UK successfully applied for membership and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> prime minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Accession_1972" title="Treaty of Accession 1972">Treaty of Accession</a> in 1972,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities_Act_1972_(UK)" title="European Communities Act 1972 (UK)">European Communities Act</a> later in the year<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and the UK <a href="/wiki/Accession_of_the_United_Kingdom_to_the_European_Communities" title="Accession of the United Kingdom to the European Communities">became a member of the EC</a> on 1 January 1973 with Denmark and Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The opposition <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> contested the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">October 1974 general election</a> with a commitment to renegotiate Britain\'s terms of membership of the EC and then hold a referendum on whether to remain in the EC on the new terms.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> After Labour won the election, the United Kingdom held its first national referendum on whether the UK should remain in the European Communities in 1975. Despite significant division within the ruling Labour Party<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> all major political parties and the mainstream press supported continuing membership of the EC. On 5 June 1975, 67.2% of the electorate and all but two<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> UK counties and regions voted to stay in;<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> support for the UK to leave the EC in 1975 appears unrelated to the support for Leave in the 2016 referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg/400px-United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg.png" width="400" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg/600px-United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg/800px-United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum%2C_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum%2C_2016.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1446" data-file-height="1191" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975_compared_to_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of results of 1975 and 2016 referendums</div></div></div>\n<p>The Labour Party campaigned in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983 general election</a> on a commitment to withdraw from the EC without a referendum,<sup id="cite_ref-foot_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foot-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> although after a heavy defeat Labour changed its policy.<sup id="cite_ref-foot_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foot-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In 1985, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Thatcher government</a> ratified the <a href="/wiki/Single_European_Act" title="Single European Act">Single European Act</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the first major revision to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome" title="Treaty of Rome">Treaty of Rome</a> \xe2\x80\x93 without a referendum.\n</p><p>In October 1990, under pressure from senior ministers and despite Margaret Thatcher\'s deep reservations, the United Kingdom joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> (ERM), with the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound sterling</a> pegged to the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">deutschmark</a>. Thatcher resigned as Prime Minister the following month, amid Conservative Party divisions arising partly from her increasingly Eurosceptic views. The United Kingdom and Italy were forced to withdraw from the ERM in September 1992, after the pound sterling and the lira came under pressure ("<a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>, the European Communities became the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> on 1 November 1993,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> reflecting the evolution of the organisation from an economic union into a political union.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Referendum_Party_and_UKIP">Referendum Party and UKIP</span></h3>\n<p>In 1994, <a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Goldsmith" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir James Goldsmith">Sir James Goldsmith</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Referendum_Party" title="Referendum Party">Referendum Party</a> to contest the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">1997 general election</a> on a platform of providing a referendum on the nature of the United Kingdom\'s relationship with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> It fielded candidates in 547 constituencies at that election, and won 810,860 votes or 2.6% of the total votes cast,<sup id="cite_ref-ref_party_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref_party-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> although it failed to win a single parliamentary seat due to its vote being spread across the country. The Referendum Party disbanded after Goldsmith\'s death in 1997.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> (UKIP), a <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Eurosceptic</a> political party, was also formed in 1993. It achieved third place in the UK during the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2004_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2004 (United Kingdom)">2004 European elections</a>, second place in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">2009 European elections</a> and first place in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2014 (United Kingdom)">2014 European elections</a>, with 27.5% of the total vote. This was the first time since the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_December_1910" title="United Kingdom general election, December 1910">1910 general election</a> that any party other than the Labour or Conservative parties had taken the largest share of the vote in a nationwide election.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> UKIP\'s electoral success in the 2014 European election has been documented as the strongest correlate of the support for the leave campaign in the 2016 referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>UKIP won two by-elections (triggered by defecting Conservative MPs) in 2014; in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a> it took 12.6% of the total vote, and held <a href="/wiki/Clacton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Clacton (UK Parliament constituency)">one of the two seats won in 2014</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Opinion_polls_1977.E2.80.932015"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_polls_1977\xe2\x80\x932015">Opinion polls 1977\xe2\x80\x932015</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opinion_polling_for_the_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="Opinion polling for the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">Opinion polling for the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum</a></div>\n<p>Both pro- and anti-EU views have had majority support at different times since 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-Mortimore_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mortimore-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">European Communities membership referendum of 1975</a>, two-thirds of British voters favoured continued EC membership.\n</p><p>In a statistical analysis published in April 2016, Professor <a href="/wiki/John_Curtice" title="John Curtice">John Curtice</a> of Strathclyde University defined <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Euroscepticism</a> as the wish to sever or reduce the powers of the EU, and conversely <a href="/wiki/Europhilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Europhilia">Europhilia</a> as the desire to preserve or increase the powers of the EU. According to this definition, the British Social Attitudes (BSA) surveys show an increase in euroscepticism from 38% (1993) to 65% (2015). Euroscepticism should, however, not be confused with the wish to leave the EU: the BSA survey for the period July\xe2\x80\x93November 2015 shows that 60% backed the option "continue as an EU member", and only 30% backed the option to "withdraw".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="May_2015_United_Kingdom_General_Election">May 2015 United Kingdom General Election</span></h2>\n<p>In the Conservative Party manifesto for the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">United Kingdom general election, 2015</a> (held on 7 May 2015), the Conservative Party offered "an EU referendum by 2017".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Referendum_of_2016">Referendum of 2016</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiations_for_EU_reform">Negotiations for EU reform</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_renegotiation_of_European_Union_membership,_2015%E2%80%9316" title="United Kingdom renegotiation of European Union membership, 2015\xe2\x80\x9316">United Kingdom renegotiation of European Union membership, 2015\xe2\x80\x9316</a></div>\n<p>In 2012, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> initially rejected calls for a referendum on the UK\'s EU membership,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> but then suggested the possibility of a future referendum to endorse his proposed renegotiation of Britain\'s relationship with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> According to the BBC, "The prime minister acknowledged the need to ensure the UK\'s [renegotiated] position within the European Union had \'the full-hearted support of the British people\' but they needed to show \'tactical and strategic patience\'."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Under pressure from many of his MPs and from the rise of UKIP, in January 2013, Cameron announced that a Conservative government would hold an in\xe2\x80\x93out referendum on EU membership before the end of 2017, on a renegotiated package, if elected in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Conservative Party won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a> with a majority. Soon afterwards the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Referendum_Act_2015" title="European Union Referendum Act 2015">European Union Referendum Act 2015</a> was introduced into Parliament to enable the referendum. Cameron favoured remaining in a reformed European Union, and sought to renegotiate on four key points: protection of the single market for non-eurozone countries, reduction of "red tape", exempting Britain from "ever-closer union", and restricting EU immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_Nov_2015_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_Nov_2015-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2015, opinion polls showed a clear majority in favour of remaining in the EU; they also showed support would drop if Cameron did not negotiate adequate safeguards for non-eurozone member states, and restrictions on benefits for EU citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The outcome of the renegotiations was announced in February 2016. Some limits to in-work benefits for new EU immigrants were agreed, but before they could be applied, a country such as the UK would have to get permission from the European Commission and then from the European Council.<sup id="cite_ref-FF1_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF1-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a speech to the House of Commons on 22 February 2016, Cameron announced a referendum date of 23 June 2016, and commented on the renegotiation settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-Hansard_22_February_2016_Column_21-25_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansard_22_February_2016_Column_21-25-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> He spoke of an intention to trigger the Article 50 process immediately following a leave vote, and of the "two-year time period to negotiate the arrangements for exit."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_groups">Campaign groups</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Campaigning_in_the_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="Campaigning in the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Campaigning in the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vote_Leave_poster,_Omagh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg/220px-Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg/330px-Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg/440px-Vote_Leave_poster%2C_Omagh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vote_Leave_poster,_Omagh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "Vote Leave" poster in <a href="/wiki/Omagh" title="Omagh">Omagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, saying "We send the EU \xc2\xa350&#160;million every day. Let\'s spend it on our NHS instead."</div></div></div>\n<p>The official campaign group for leaving the EU was <a href="/wiki/Vote_Leave" title="Vote Leave">Vote Leave</a><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> after a contest for the designation with <a href="/wiki/Leave.EU" title="Leave.EU">Leave.EU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The official campaign to stay in the EU, chaired by <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Rose" title="Stuart Rose">Stuart Rose</a>, was known as <a href="/wiki/Britain_Stronger_in_Europe" title="Britain Stronger in Europe">Britain Stronger in Europe</a>, or informally as \'Remain\'. Other campaigns supporting remaining in the EU included Conservatives In,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Labour_in_for_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour in for Britain">Labour in for Britain</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> #INtogether (Liberal Democrats),<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Greens for a Better Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scientists_for_EU" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientists for EU">Scientists for EU</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Environmentalists For Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Universities for Europe<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and Another Europe is Possible.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Referendum_result">Referendum result</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Results_of_the_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="Results of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Results of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016</a></div>\n<p>The result was announced on the morning of 24 June: 51.89% voted in favour of leaving the European Union, and 48.11% voted in favour of remaining a member of the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC24forecast_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC24forecast-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Comprehensive results are available from the UK Electoral Commission Referendum Results site. A <a href="/wiki/EU_Referendum_Rules_triggering_a_2nd_EU_Referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="EU Referendum Rules triggering a 2nd EU Referendum">petition calling for a second referendum</a> attracted more than four million signatures,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> but was rejected by the government on 9 July.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffc010; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Remain</div></div></div></div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_analysis_of_voters">Demographic analysis of voters</span></h3>\n<p>According to Thomas Sampson, an economist at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a>, "Older and less-educated voters were more likely to vote \'leave\'... A majority of white voters wanted to leave, but only 33 percent of Asian voters and 27 percent of black voters chose leave. There was no gender split in the vote, with 52 percent of both men and women voting to leave. Leaving the European Union received support from across the political spectrum... Voting to leave the European Union was strongly associated with holding socially conservative political beliefs, opposing cosmopolitanism, and thinking life in Britain is getting worse rather than better."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>&#160;Econometric studies show "first, education and, to a lesser extent, age were the strongest demographic predictors of voting behavior... Second, poor economic outcomes at the individual or area level were associated with voting to leave... Third, support for leaving the European Union is strongly associated with self-reported opposition to immigration, but not with exposure to immigration."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Resignations.2C_contests.2C_and_appointments"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Resignations,_contests,_and_appointments">Resignations, contests, and appointments</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="Aftermath of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Aftermath of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016</a></div>\n<p>After the result was declared, Cameron announced that he would resign by October.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> He stood down on 13 July 2016, with <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> becoming Prime Minister after a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2016" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 2016">leadership contest</a>. <a href="/wiki/George_Osborne" title="George Osborne">George Osborne</a> was replaced as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hammond" title="Philip Hammond">Philip Hammond</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a> <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> was appointed <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Davis_(British_politician)" title="David Davis (British politician)">David Davis</a> became <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" title="Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union">Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union</a>. <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a> lost a vote of confidence among his parliamentary party, and <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2016" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 2016">an unsuccessful leadership challenge</a> was launched. On 4 July, <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a> announced his resignation as leader of UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irregularities">Irregularities</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russian_interference_in_the_Brexit_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian interference in the Brexit referendum">Russian interference in the Brexit referendum</a></div>\n<p>On 11 May 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_Kingdom)" title="Electoral Commission (United Kingdom)">Electoral Commission</a> found against Leave.EU, which ran a separate campaign to the official pro-Brexit group Vote Leave, following its investigations into alleged irregularities during the referendum campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Leave.EU\'s co-founder <a href="/wiki/Arron_Banks" title="Arron Banks">Arron Banks</a> has stated that he rejects the outcome of the investigation and will be challenging it in court.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2018, the <a href="/wiki/UK_Electoral_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Electoral Commission">UK Electoral Commission</a> found <a href="/wiki/Vote_Leave" title="Vote Leave">Vote Leave</a> to have <a href="/wiki/Vote_Leave#Strategy" title="Vote Leave">broken electoral law</a>, spending over its limit.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Also, the House of Commons Culture, Media and Sport Select Committee released an interim report on <i>Disinformation and \xe2\x80\x98fake news\xe2\x80\x99</i>, stating that the largest donor in the Brexit campaign, <a href="/wiki/Arron_Banks" title="Arron Banks">Arron Banks</a>, used money from UK sources, and may have been financed by the Russian government.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> This has led to litigation to declare the result is void.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Procedure_for_leaving_the_EU">Procedure for leaving the EU</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_invocation_of_Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union" title="United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf/page1-200px-Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf.jpg" width="200" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf/page1-300px-Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf/page1-400px-Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1240" data-file-height="1753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prime_Ministers_letter_to_European_Council_President_Donald_Tusk.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Letter from Theresa May invoking Article 50</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union" title="Withdrawal from the European Union">Withdrawal from the European Union</a> is governed by <a href="/wiki/Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Article_50_invocation_procedure" class="mw-redirect" title="Article 50 invocation procedure">Article 50 invocation procedure</a>, a member notifies the <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a>, whereupon the EU is required to <i>negotiate and conclude an agreement with [the leaving] State, setting out the arrangements for its withdrawal, taking account of the framework for its future relationship with the [European] Union</i>. The negotiation period is limited to two years unless extended, after which the treaties cease to apply.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> There was a discussion whether parallel negotiation of withdrawal terms and future relationships under Article 50 are appropriate (Chancellor Merkel\'s initial view) or whether Britain did not have the right to negotiate future trade with the EU27 as this power is arguably reserved to the EU as long as the UK is a member (the view of a European Commission lawyer).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the 2015 Referendum Act did not expressly require Article 50 to be invoked,<sup id="cite_ref-constunit_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constunit-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> the UK government stated that it would expect a leave vote to be followed by withdrawal.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>  Following the referendum result, Cameron resigned and said that it would be for the incoming Prime Minister to invoke Article 50.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Supreme Court of the United Kingdom">Supreme Court</a> ruled in <a href="/wiki/R_(Miller_and_Dos_Santos)_v_Secretary_of_State_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="R (Miller and Dos Santos) v Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union">the Miller case</a> in January 2017 that the government needed parliamentary approval to trigger Article 50.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>  Subsequently, the House of Commons overwhelmingly voted, on 1 February 2017, for a government bill authorising the prime minister to invoke Article 50,<sup id="cite_ref-house-trigger_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-house-trigger-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and the bill passed into law as the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Notification_of_Withdrawal)_Act_2017" title="European Union (Notification of Withdrawal) Act 2017">European Union (Notification of Withdrawal) Act 2017</a>. Theresa May then signed a letter invoking Article 50 on 28 March 2017, which was delivered on 29 March by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Barrow" title="Tim Barrow">Tim Barrow</a>, the UK\'s ambassador to the EU, to European Council President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Tusk" title="Donald Tusk">Donald Tusk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Brexit_letter_28_03_2017_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Brexit_letter_28_03_2017-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stamp_Hunt_2017_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stamp_Hunt_2017-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reversibility">Reversibility</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_invocation_of_Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union#Reversibility" title="United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union \xc2\xa7&#160;Reversibility</a></div>\n<p>It has been argued that the Article 50 withdrawal process may be halted unilaterally by the British government,<sup id="cite_ref-:5_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> with which opinion the author of Article 50 itself, <a href="/wiki/John_Kerr,_Baron_Kerr_of_Kinlochard" title="John Kerr, Baron Kerr of Kinlochard">Lord Kerr</a>, has expressed agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The European Parliament\'s Brexit committee has noted that unilateral revocation, regardless of its legality, poses a substantial <a href="/wiki/Moral_hazard" title="Moral hazard">moral hazard</a>, with EU member states potentially able to abuse it to blackmail the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-EU_Parliament_point_of_no_return2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU_Parliament_point_of_no_return2-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_of_Brexit">Date of Brexit</span></h3>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:\n<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Consolidated_version_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union/Title_VI:_Final_Provisions#Article_50" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Consolidated version of the Treaty on European Union/Title VI: Final Provisions">The Withdrawal Clause</a></b></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Both parties to the withdrawal negotiation are bound by <a href="/wiki/Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union#Provisions" class="mw-redirect" title="Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">Article 50 (3)</a>, which states explicitly that the EU treaties will cease to apply "from the date of entry into force of the withdrawal agreement or, failing that, two years after" the withdrawal notification unless the EU Council and UK agree to extend the two-year period.\n</p><p>On the EU side, the EU\'s <i>Directives for the negotiation of an agreement</i> notes that "The Agreement should set a withdrawal date which is at the latest 30 March 2019 at 00:00 (<a href="/wiki/Time_zone" title="Time zone">Brussels time</a>)," \xe2\x80\x93 i.e. <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Time" title="Central European Time">Central European Time</a> \xe2\x80\x93  "unless the European Council, in agreement with the United Kingdom, unanimously decides to extend this period in accordance with Article 50(3) of the Treaty on European Union."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the British side, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Act_2018" title="European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018">European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018</a>, section 20(1) defines "exit day" as "29 March 2019 at 11.00 p.m".\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiations">Negotiations</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brexit_negotiations" title="Brexit negotiations">Brexit negotiations</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timing">Timing</span></h3>\n<p>The British and EU negotiators agreed that initial negotiations, relating especially to residency rights, would commence in June 2017 (immediately after the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2017" title="French presidential election, 2017">French presidential</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2017" title="French legislative election, 2017">parliamentary elections</a>), and full negotiations, relating especially to trading agreements, could commence in October 2017 (immediately after the <a href="/wiki/German_federal_election,_2017" title="German federal election, 2017">German federal election, 2017</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-uk-caves_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-uk-caves-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The first day of talks was 19 June.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-uk-caves_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-uk-caves-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3>\n<p>On 28 June 2016, Chancellor of Germany <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>, and on the following day European Council President Tusk, stated that the UK could remain in the <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">European Single Market</a> (ESM) only if the UK accepted its four freedoms of movement: for goods, capital, services, and labour.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In October, Prime Minister Theresa May emphasised that ending the jurisdiction of EU law and free movement from Europe were the UK\'s priorities, along with British and EU companies having maximum freedom to trade in the UK and the ESM.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2016, May proposed that Britain and the other EU countries mutually guarantee the residency rights of the 3.3&#160;million EU immigrants in Britain and those of the 1.2&#160;million British citizens living on the Continent, in order to exclude their fates being bargained during Brexit negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Despite initial approval from a majority of EU states, May\'s proposal was blocked by Tusk and Merkel.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2017, the Prime Minister presented 12 negotiating objectives and confirmed that the UK government would not seek permanent <a href="/wiki/British_membership_of_the_European_Economic_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="British membership of the European Economic Area">single market membership</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The European Parliament\'s lead negotiator <a href="/wiki/Guy_Verhofstadt" title="Guy Verhofstadt">Guy Verhofstadt</a> responded that there could be no "cherry-picking" by the UK in the talks.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The statutory period for negotiation began on 29 March 2017, when the UK formally submitted a letter notifying withdrawal. The letter called for a "deep and special relationship" between the UK and the EU, and warned that failure to reach an agreement would result in EU-UK trade under World Trade Organisation terms, and a weakening of the UK\'s co-operation in the fight against crime and terrorism. The letter suggested prioritising an early deal on the rights of EU citizens in the UK and vice versa, and stated that the UK would not seek to remain within the ESM. Instead, the UK would seek a free trade agreement with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In response, Merkel insisted that the EU would not discuss future co-operation without first settling the terms of leaving the EU; Verhofstadt referred to the letter as "blackmail" with regard to the point on security and terrorism, and EU Commission president <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Juncker" title="Jean-Claude Juncker">Jean-Claude Juncker</a> said the UK\'s decision to quit the block was a "choice they will regret one day".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 29 April 2017, immediately after the first round of French presidential elections, the EU27 heads of state accepted negotiating guidelines prepared by Tusk.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The guidelines take the view that Article 50 permits a two-phased negotiation, in which the UK first agrees to a financial commitment and to lifelong benefits for EU citizens in Britain, and then negotiations on a future relationship can begin.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In the first phase, the EU27 would demand the UK pay a "divorce bill", initially estimated as amounting to \xc2\xa352bn<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and then, after additional financial demands from Germany, France, and Poland, to \xc2\xa392bn.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> A report of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Committee" title="European Union Committee">European Union Committee</a> of the House of Lords, published on 4 March 2017, stated that if there is no post-Brexit deal at the end of the negotiating period, the UK could withdraw without payment.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 May 2017, the European Council authorised its negotiators to start the Brexit talks and it adopted its negotiating directives.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The first day of talks took place on 19 June, where Davis and <a href="/wiki/Michel_Barnier" title="Michel Barnier">Michel Barnier</a>, European Chief Negotiator for Brexit, agreed to prioritise the question of residency rights, while Davis conceded that a discussion of the Northern Irish border would have to await future trade agreements.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 June 2017, Prime Minister May guaranteed that no EU citizen living legally in the UK would be forced to leave, and offered that any EU citizen who lived in the UK for more than five years until an unspecified deadline between March 2017 and March 2019 would enjoy the same rights as a UK citizen, conditional on the EU providing the same offer to British expatriates living in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The Prime Minister detailed her residency proposals on 26 June, but drew no concessions from EU negotiators,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> who had declined to expedite agreement on expatriates by the end of June 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and who are hoping for European courts to continue to have jurisdiction in the UK with regards to EU citizens, according to their negotiation aims published in May 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second round of negotiations began in mid-July 2017. Progress was made on the Northern Irish border question; UK negotiators requested a detailed breakdown of the "divorce bill" demand; and the EU negotiators criticised the UK\'s citizenship rights offer.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> David Davis did not commit to a net payment by the UK to the EU with regards to the requested divorce bill, while Michel Barnier would not compromise on his demand for the European Court of Justice to have continuing jurisdiction over the rights of EU citizens living in the UK after Brexit,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> rejecting the compromise proposal of a new international body made up of British and EU judges.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 August 2017, the UK government disclosed the first of several papers detailing British ambitions following Brexit, discussing trade and customs arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> On 23 August, Theresa May announced that Britain will leave the EU Court of Justice\'s direct jurisdiction when the Brexit transition period that is planned after March 2019 ends, but that both the British courts and the EU Court of Justice will also keep "half an eye" on each other\'s rulings afterwards as well.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> One of the UK government\'s position papers published in August called for no additional restrictions for goods already on the market in the UK and EU.<sup id="cite_ref-auto5_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The third round of negotiations began on 28 August 2017. There was disagreement over the financial settlement; The <i>Irish Times</i> explained that British negotiators referred to the seven-year <a href="/wiki/Multiannual_Financial_Framework" title="Multiannual Financial Framework">Multiannual Financial Framework</a> (MFF or Maff) for the period 2014\xe2\x80\x932020 agreed by member states and the EU parliament as a "planning tool" for the next period rather than a legally-binding financial obligation on member states. The British case is that the MFF sets ceilings on spending under various headings and is later radically revised during the annual budget process when real legal obligations on each state arises. This contrasts with the EU Commission\'s methodology for calculating the UK Brexit bill which involves dividing the MFF into the shares historically agreed by each member state.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> On the Irish border question there was a "breakthrough", with the British side guaranteeing free movement of EU citizens within the <a href="/wiki/Common_travel_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Common travel area">Common travel area</a> constituting Ireland and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 5 September 2017, Davis said that "concrete progress" had been made over the summer in areas such as protecting the rights of British expats in the EU to access healthcare and over the future of the Irish border, while significant differences over the "divorce bill" remained.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> On 9 September, the EU Commission published several negotiating papers, including one in which the EU concedes/declares that it is the responsibility of the UK to propose solutions for the post-Brexit Irish border. The paper envisages that a "unique" solution would be permissible here; in other words, any such exceptional Irish solution would not necessarily be a template for post-Brexit relationships with the other EU members.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 September 2017, May announced further details of her Brexit proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-youtubesep21_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-youtubesep21-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sep21_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep21-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In addition to offering 20&#160;billion euros over a two-year transition period and continued acceptance of European immigrants,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> she also offered a "bold new security relationship" with the EU which would be "unprecedented in its depth" and to continue to make "an ongoing contribution" to projects considered greatly to the EU and UK\'s advantage, such as science and security projects.<sup id="cite_ref-sep21_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep21-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-youtubesep21_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-youtubesep21-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> She also confirmed that the UK would not "stand in the way" of Juncker\'s proposals for further EU integration.<sup id="cite_ref-sep21_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep21-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-youtubesep21_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-youtubesep21-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Barnier welcomed May\'s proposal as "constructive,"<sup id="cite_ref-brexitoct18_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brexitoct18-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> but that it also "must be translated into negotiating positions to make meaningful progress".<sup id="cite_ref-brexitoct18_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brexitoct18-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, President of France <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> was adamant that the EU would not begin negotiations on future EU-UK relationships until "the regulation of European citizens, the financial terms of the exit, and the questions of Ireland" were "clarified" by the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fourth round of talks began on 25 September, with Barnier declaring he had no mandate from the EU27 to discuss a transition deal suggested by Prime Minister May. Davis reiterated that the UK could honour commitments made during its EU membership only in the context of a future "special partnership" deal with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_Council_meetings#2010\xe2\x80\x93present" title="List of European Council meetings">European Council meeting</a> of 19/20 October 2017, the 27 leaders of the EU states were to decide whether or not to start trade negotiations with the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-auto5_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> However, Davis has conceded that so soon after the German elections on 24 September, a German coalition government may not be in place in time for making this decision in October, delaying any European Council decision until their December meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>EU negotiators have stated that an agreement must be reached between Britain and the EU by October 2018 in order to leave time for national parliaments to endorse Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-brexitoct18_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brexitoct18-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 9 October 2017, May announced to the British Parliament that Britain could operate as an "independent trading nation" after Brexit if no trade deal is reached with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2017, EU leaders announced an agreement to begin the next phase of negotiations, with talks on a transition period after March 2019 to begin in early 2018 and discussions on the future UK-EU relationship, including trade and security, to begin in March.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After elections in March 2018, the Italian president appointed a eurosceptic Italian government on 1 June 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> a development expected to affect the Brexit outcome.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 10 June 2018, the Irish Prime Minister Leo Varadkar cleared the path for the June negotiations by postponing the Irish border question until the final Brexit deal in October 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 19 June 2018, the UK and the EU published a joint statement outlining agreements at the negotiators\' level. Michel Barnier praised the "dedication and commitment" of the negotiating teams, and said progress had been made in issues like customs, VAT and the European nuclear agreement, Euratom.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-UK-EUProgressReportJun2018_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-UK-EUProgressReportJun2018-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UK-EUProgressReportJun2018_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UK-EUProgressReportJun2018-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Article_50_British_legislation">Post-Article 50 British legislation</span></h2>\n<h3><span id="European_Union_.28Withdrawal.29_Act_2018"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Act_2018">European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Act_2018" title="European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018">European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018</a></div>\n<p>In October 2016, Theresa May promised a "Great Repeal Bill", which would repeal the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities_Act_1972_(UK)" title="European Communities Act 1972 (UK)">European Communities Act 1972</a> and restate in UK law all enactments previously in force under EU law. Subsequently renamed the European Union (Withdrawal) bill, it was introduced to the House of Commons on 13 July 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 September 2017, the bill passed its first vote and second reading by a margin of 326 votes to 290 votes in the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The bill was further amended on a series of votes in both Houses of Parliament. After the Act became law on 26 June 2018, the European Council decided on 29 June to renew its call on Member States and Union institutions to step up their work on preparedness at all levels and for all outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Withdrawal Act fixes the period ending 21 January 2019 for the government to decide on how to proceed if the negotiations have not reached agreement in principle on both the withdrawal arrangements and the framework for the future relationship between the UK and EU; while, alternatively, making future ratification of the withdrawal agreement as a treaty between the UK and EU depend upon the prior enactment of another act of Parliament for approving the final terms of withdrawal when the current <a href="/wiki/Brexit_negotiations" title="Brexit negotiations">Brexit negotiations</a> are completed.
