In any event, the act does not alter the two-year period for negotiating allowed by Article 50 that ends at the latest on 29 March 2019 if the UK has not by then ratified a withdrawal agreement.\n</p><p>The Withdrawal Act which became law in June 2018 allows for various outcomes including no negotiated settlement.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_government_bills">Additional government bills</span></h3>\n<p>A report published in March 2017 by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Government" title="Institute for Government">Institute for Government</a> commented that, in addition to the European Union (Withdrawal) bill, primary and secondary legislation will be needed to cover the gaps in policy areas such as customs, immigration and agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The report also commented that the role of the devolved legislatures was unclear, and could cause problems, and as many as fifteen new additional Brexit Bills may be required, which would involve strict prioritisation and limiting Parliamentary time for in-depth examination of new legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016 and 2017, the House of Lords published a series of reports on Brexit-related subjects, including:\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/72/72.pdf">Brexit: the options for trade</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/76/76.pdf">Brexit: UK-Irish relations</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/77/77.pdf">Brexit: future UK-EU security and police cooperation</a></li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/78/78.pdf">Brexit: fisheries</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/109/109.pdf">Brexit: environment and climate change</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/136/136.pdf">Brexit: the Crown Dependencies</a></li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/134/134.pdf">Brexit: justice for families, individuals and businesses?</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/135/135.pdf">Brexit: trade in non- financial services</a></li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Euratom">Euratom</span></h4>\n<p>The Nuclear Safeguards Bill 2017\xe2\x80\x9319, relating to withdrawal from <a href="/wiki/Euratom" class="mw-redirect" title="Euratom">Euratom</a>, was presented to Parliament in October 2017 and began its Report Stage in January 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voting_on_the_final_outcome">Voting on the final outcome</span></h3>\n<p>Replying to questions at a parliamentary committee about Parliament\'s involvement in voting on the outcome of the negotiations with the EU, the Prime Minister said that "delivering on the vote of the British people to leave the European Union" was her priority. The shadow Brexit secretary, <a href="/wiki/Keir_Starmer" title="Keir Starmer">Keir Starmer</a>, commented that the government did not want a vote at the beginning of the process, to trigger Article 50, nor a vote at the end.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Developments_since_the_Referendum_of_2016">Developments since the Referendum of 2016</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span></h3>\n<p>Opinion polls in the fortnight following the referendum suggested that the immediate reaction in the Netherlands and other European countries was a decline in support for Eurosceptic movements.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A general election was held on 8 June 2017, announced at short notice by the new Prime Minister Theresa May. The Conservative Party, Labour and UKIP made manifesto pledges to implement the referendum, although the Labour manifesto differed in its approach to Brexit negotiations, such as unilaterally offering permanent residence to EU immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The Liberal Democrat Party and the Green Party manifestos proposed a policy of remaining in the EU via a second referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish Nationalist Party manifesto proposed a policy of waiting for the outcome of the Brexit negotiations and then holding a referendum on Scottish independence.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Compared to the 2015 general election, the Conservatives gained votes (but nevertheless lost seats and their majority in the House of Commons). Labour gained significantly on votes and seats, retaining its position as the second-largest party. The DUP and Sinn F\xc3\xa9in also made gains in votes and seats. Parties losing votes included the SNP, Liberals, Greens, and especially UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 June 2017, Conservatives and the DUP reached a <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> agreement whereby the DUP would back the Conservatives in key votes in the House of Commons over the course of the parliament. The agreement included additional funding of \xc2\xa31 billion for Northern Ireland, highlighted mutual support for Brexit and national security, expressed commitment to the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>, and indicated that policies such as the state pension triple lock and <a href="/wiki/Winter_Fuel_Payment" title="Winter Fuel Payment">winter fuel payments</a> would be maintained.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h3>\n<p>Six weeks after the referendum, the Bank of England sought to cushion the potential shock to the economy by lowering interest rates to the record low of 0.25%, and by creating 70 billion pounds of new money, thereby depreciating the pound and encouraging commercial banks to pass on lower borrowing costs.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A year-long "wage squeeze" attributed to the referendum ended in February 2018, with wage growth catching up with inflation. Inflation had gradually risen to 3% before receding again. Since the referendum, absolute employment has continuously risen to previously unrecorded levels, and by early 2018 relative unemployment reached its lowest level (4.2%) recorded since 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 2017 the UK continued to be the favourite European destination for foreign physical investment (as distinct from company takeovers), creating 50,000 new jobs, ahead of Germany (31,000 jobs) and France. Factors mentioned were sterling devaluation since the referendum, broadband, and American investment.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span></h3>\n<p>Official figures for June 2017 (published in February 2018) showed that net EU immigration to the UK had slowed to about 100,000 immigrants per year, corresponding to the immigration level of 2014. Meanwhile, immigration from non-EU countries had increased. Taken together, the two inflows into the UK result in an only slightly reduced net immigration of 230,000 newcomers in the year to June 2017. The Head of the Office of National Statistics suggested that Brexit could well be a factor for the slowdown in EU immigration, but cautioned there might be other reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_impact_on_the_United_Kingdom">Domestic impact on the United Kingdom</span></h2>\n<p>The Department for Exiting the European Union (DExEU) produced reports on the economic impact on 58 industries of Britain leaving the EU. The Labour Party made a freedom of information request for details about the reports, but DExEU said that publishing the information would undermine policy formulation, and that it needed to carry out policymaking in a "safe space".<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Labour then proposed a motion of a rarely-used type known as a "<a href="/wiki/Humble_address" title="Humble address">humble address</a>" in the Commons on 1 November 2017, calling for the papers to be released; the motion was passed unanimously. The leader of the house, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Leadsom" title="Andrea Leadsom">Andrea Leadsom</a>, said that there could be some delay while ministers decided how to release the information without prejudicing Brexit negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration_2">Immigration</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_term">Long term</span></h4>\n<p>Immigration was cited as the second-most important reason for those voting to Leave. <a href="/wiki/KPMG" title="KPMG">KPMG</a>, based on a 2017 survey of 2,000 EU workers in the UK, estimated that about a million EU citizens working in the UK saw their future in Britain as over or hanging in the balance.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2017 paper by King\'s College London economists Giuseppe Forte and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Portes" title="Jonathan Portes">Jonathan Portes</a> found that "while future migration flows will be driven by a number of factors, macroeconomic and otherwise, Brexit and the end of free movement will result in a large fall in immigration from EEA countries to the UK."<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2016 study by Portes, "The spectrum of options for UK immigration policy post Brexit remains wide... However, almost any plausible outcome will result in an increase in regulatory burdens on business; a reduction in the flows of both unskilled and skilled workers; and an increase in illegal working. The key question for policymakers will be how to minimise these negative impacts while at the same time addressing domestic political demands for increased control without antagonising our EU partners to the point of prejudicing other key aspects of the negotiations. This will not be an easy task."<sup id="cite_ref-:18_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Will Somerville of the Migration Policy Institute wrote that "Future migration levels are impossible to predict in the absence of policy and economic certainty", but estimated immediately after the referendum that the UK "would continue to receive 500,000 or more immigrants (from EU and non-EU countries taken together) per year, with annual net migration around 200,000".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The decline in EEA immigration is likely to have an adverse impact on the British health sector.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>New York Times</i>, Brexit "seems certain" to make it harder and costlier for the NHS, which already suffers from chronic understaffing, to recruit nurses, midwives and doctors from the rest of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immediate_effects">Immediate effects</span></h4>\n<p>Official figures in March 2017 indicated that EU immigration to the UK continued to exceed emigration, but the difference between immigration and emigration ("net migration") had fallen to its lowest for three years.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The number of EU nurses registering with the NHS fell from 1,304 in July 2016 to 46 in April 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_effects_of_Brexit" title="Economic effects of Brexit">Economic effects of Brexit</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immediate_effects_2">Immediate effects</span></h4>\n<p>Research on the effects that have already materialised in the United Kingdom since the referendum results show that the referendum result pushed up UK inflation by 1.7 percentage points, leading to an annual cost of \xc2\xa3404 for the average British household.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Another study on the effects that had already materialised found that by September 2018, the economic costs of the Brexit vote were already 2% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:17_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> A September 2018 analysis by the think tank Centre for European Reform showed that the losses amounted to 2.5% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-:20_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to a <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> analysis, the Brexit referendum results had by December 2017 reduced national British income by between 0.6% and 1.3%, which amounts to almost \xc2\xa3350&#160;million a week.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Barry_Eichengreen" title="Barry Eichengreen">Barry Eichengreen</a> noted in August 2017 that some of the adverse effects of uncertainty brought about by the Brexit referendum were being made apparent, as British consumer confidence was down and spending had declined to its lowest level in four years.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In November 2017, it was reported that European banks had reduced their UK-related assets by \xe2\x82\xac350bn in the 12 months after Brexit vote,&#160;and that the trend was expected to increase ahead of the March 2019 Brexit deadline.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_economic_analyses">Long-term economic analyses</span></h4>\n<p>There is overwhelming or near-unanimous agreement among economists that leaving the European Union will adversely affect the British economy in the medium- and long-term.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> Surveys of economists in 2016 showed overwhelming agreement that Brexit would likely reduce the UK\'s real per-capita income level.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:7_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 survey of the existing academic literature found "the research literature displays a broad consensus that in the long run Brexit will make the United Kingdom poorer because it will create new barriers to trade, foreign direct investment, and immigration. However, there is substantial uncertainty over how large the effect will be, with plausible estimates of the cost ranging between 1 and 10 percent of the UK\'s income per capita."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> These estimates differ depending on whether the UK stays in the European Single Market (for instance, by joining the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">EEA</a>), makes a free trade agreement with the EU, or reverts to the trade rules that govern relations between all World Trade Organization members.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In January 2018, the UK government\'s own Brexit analysis was leaked; it showed that UK economic growth would be stunted by 2\xe2\x80\x938% for at least 15 years following secession from the EU, depending on the leave scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most economists, including the UK Treasury, argue that being in the EU has a strong positive effect on trade and as a result the UK\'s trade would be worse off if it left the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> According to a group of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> economists, under a "hard Brexit" whereby the UK reverts to WTO rules, one-third of UK exports to the EU would be tariff-free, one-quarter would face high trade barriers and other exports risk tariffs in the range of 1\xe2\x80\x9310%.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 study based on data from 2010 found that "almost all UK regions are systematically more vulnerable to Brexit than regions in any other country. Due to their longstanding trade integration with the UK, Irish regions have levels of Brexit exposure, which are similar to those of the UK regions with the lowest levels of exposure, namely London and northern parts of Scotland. Meanwhile, the other most risk-exposed EU regions are all in southern Germany, with levels of risk which are typically half that of any UK or Irish region, and one third of that displayed by many UK regions. There is also a very noticeable economic geography logic to the levels of exposure with north-western European regions typically being the most exposed to Brexit, while regions in southern and eastern Europe are barely affected at all by Brexit, at least in terms of the trade linkages... Overall, the UK is far more exposed to Brexit risks than the rest of the EU."<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the referendum, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Fiscal_Studies" title="Institute for Fiscal Studies">Institute for Fiscal Studies</a> published a report funded by the <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_Social_Research_Council" title="Economic and Social Research Council">Economic and Social Research Council</a> which warned that Britain would lose up to \xc2\xa370&#160;billion in reduced economic growth if it did not retain <a href="/wiki/Single_Market" class="mw-redirect" title="Single Market">Single Market</a> membership, with new trade deals unable to make up the difference.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> One of these areas is financial services, which are helped by EU-wide "passporting" for financial products, which an <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wyman" title="Oliver Wyman">Oliver Wyman</a> report for a pro-EU lobby group estimated indirectly accounted for up to 71,000 jobs and \xc2\xa310&#160;billion of tax annually,<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (November 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and some banks announced plans to relocate some of their operations outside the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2016 article by <a href="/wiki/John_Armour" title="John Armour">John Armour</a>, Professor of Law and Finance at Oxford University, "a \'soft\' Brexit, whereby the UK leaves the EU but remains in the single market, would be a lower-risk option for the City than other Brexit options, because it would enable financial services firms to continue to rely on regulatory passporting rights."<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2017 study found, on the basis of "plausible, empirically based estimates of the likely impacts on growth and wages using relationships from the existing empirical literature", that "Brexit-induced reductions in migration are likely to have a significant negative impact on UK GDP&#160;<i>per capita</i> (and GDP), with marginal positive impacts on wages in the low-skill service sector."<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It is unclear how changes in trade and foreign investment will interact with immigration, but these changes are likely to be important.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Former Governor of the Bank of England <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_King,_Baron_King_of_Lothbury" title="Mervyn King, Baron King of Lothbury">Mervyn King</a> commented that warnings of economic doom regarding leaving the EU were overstated and that the UK should leave the single market and probably the customs union in order to gain more opportunities, which would lead to improved British economic performance.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Short-term_economic_analyses">Short-term economic analyses</span></h4>\n<p>Short-term macroeconomic forecasts by the Bank of England and other banks of what would happen immediately after the Brexit referendum proved to be too pessimistic.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The assessments assumed that the referendum results would create greater uncertainty on financial markets and in business and reduce consumer confidence more than it did.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> According to Oxford University economist <a href="/wiki/Simon_Wren-Lewis" title="Simon Wren-Lewis">Simon Wren-Lewis</a>, "short term unconditional macroeconomic forecasts are extremely unreliable" and they are something that academic economists do not do, unlike banks.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Wren-Lewis notes that long-term projections of the impact of Brexit, on the other hand, have a strong empirical foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Barry_Eichengreen" title="Barry Eichengreen">Barry Eichengreen</a> wrote that economists "have had little success at reliably predicting when and why uncertainty arises"&#160;and that it is unclear how severe the impact of uncertainty actually is.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> King\'s College London economist Jonathan Portes said that "short-term economic forecasting is very unreliable", and compared short-term economic forecasts to weather forecasts and the long-term economic forecasts to climate forecasts: the methodologies used in long-term forecasts are "well-established and robust".<sup id="cite_ref-:13_221-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Other economists note that central bank forecasts are not intended for pinpoint accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_221-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> economist Thomas Sampson notes that it is harder to assess the short-term impact that the transition process to Brexit will have, but that long-term assessments of the post-Brexit period are more reliable.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Financial Times</i>, economists are in agreement that the short-term effects are uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_200-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 5 January 2017 <a href="/wiki/Andy_Haldane" title="Andy Haldane">Andy Haldane</a>, the Chief Economist and the Executive Director of Monetary Analysis and Statistics at the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, said that the BoE\'s own forecast predicting an immediate economic downturn due to the referendum result was inaccurate and noted strong market performance immediately after the referendum,<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> although some have pointed to prices rising faster than wages.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Haldane said that the field of economics was "to some degree in crisis" because of its failure to predict the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x932008">financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x932008</a>, and added that the Brexit economic forecast was only inaccurate in its near-term assessment, and that over time, the Bank still expected that Brexit would harm economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Imperial College London economist David Miles responded to Haldane, saying that there was no crisis in economics, and that economists did not purport to be able to forecast with full certainty or predict the precise timing of events.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Miles said that it was widely acknowledged among economists that short-term forecasts, such as the BoE\'s, are unreliable.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_agencies">Loss of agencies</span></h4>\n<p>Brexit requires relocating the offices and staff of the <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">European Medicines Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Banking_Authority" title="European Banking Authority">European Banking Authority</a>, currently based in London.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> The agencies together employ more than 1,000 people and will respectively relocate to <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The EU is also considering restricting the <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">clearing</a> of euro-denominated trades to <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a> jurisdictions, which would end London\'s dominance in this sector.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education_and_academic_research">Higher education and academic research</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brexit_and_arrangements_for_science_and_technology" title="Brexit and arrangements for science and technology">Brexit and arrangements for science and technology</a></div>\n<p>According to a 2016 study by Ken Mayhew, Emeritus Professor of Education and Economic Performance at Oxford University, Brexit poses the following threats to higher education: "loss of research funding from EU sources; loss of students from other EU countries; the impact on the ability of the sector to hire academic staff from EU countries; and the impact on the ability of UK students to study abroad."<sup id="cite_ref-:19_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The UK received more from the EU for research than it contributed<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> with universities getting just over 10% of their research income from the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> All funding for net beneficiaries from the EU, including universities, was guaranteed by the government in August 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Before the funding announcement, a newspaper investigation reported that some research projects were reluctant to include British researchers due to uncertainties over funding.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Currently the UK is part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Research_Area" title="European Research Area">European Research Area</a> and the UK is likely to wish to remain an associated member.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg/220px-Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg/330px-Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg/440px-Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicola_Sturgeon_addresses_journalists_over_Brexit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister of Scotland</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Sturgeon" title="Nicola Sturgeon">Nicola Sturgeon</a> addresses journalists over Brexit and Scotland\'s place within Europe at <a href="/wiki/Bute_House" title="Bute House">Bute House</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As suggested by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> before the referendum,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister of Scotland</a> announced that officials were planning <a href="/wiki/Proposed_second_Scottish_independence_referendum" title="Proposed second Scottish independence referendum">an independence referendum</a> due to the result of Scotland voting to remain in the European Union when England and Wales voted to leave.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> In March 2017, the SNP leader and First Minister <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Sturgeon" title="Nicola Sturgeon">Nicola Sturgeon</a> requested a second Scottish independence referendum in 2018 or 2019 (before Britain\'s formal exit from the EU).<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> The UK Prime Minister immediately rejected the requested timing, but not the referendum itself.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The referendum was approved by the Scottish Parliament on 28 March 2017. Sturgeon called for a "phased return" of an independent Scotland back to the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the referendum, First Minister Sturgeon suggested that Scotland might refuse <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Consent_Motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative Consent Motion">consent</a> for legislation required to leave the EU,<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> though some lawyers argue that Scotland cannot block Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 21 March 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/UK_Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union_(Legal_Continuity)_(Scotland)_Act_2018" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Withdrawal from the European Union (Legal Continuity) (Scotland) Act 2018">Scottish Continuity Bill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Herald_Scotland_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herald_Scotland-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> This was passed due to stalling negotiations between the Scottish Government and the British Government on where powers within devolved policy areas should lie after exit day from the European Union.  This Act allows for all devolved policy areas to remain within the remit of the Scottish Parliament and reduces the executive power upon exit day that the UK Withdrawal Bill provides for Ministers of the Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> The Bill gained Royal Assent on 28 April 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-Herald_Scotland_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herald_Scotland-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_Brexit_on_bilateral_UK_relations">Impact of Brexit on bilateral UK relations</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_agreements">International agreements</span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Financial Times</i> said that there were approximately 759 international agreements, spanning 168 non-EU countries, that the UK would no longer be a party to upon leaving the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> This figure does not include <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="World Trade Organisation">World Trade Organisation</a> or <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> opt-in accords, and excludes "narrow agreements", which may also have to be renegotiated.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Aviation may be heavily affected. The EU has rules allowing its airlines to fly anywhere in the union, also domestic, which will not apply to the UK anymore. The British airline <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a> decided to relocate its headquarter. The EU also has treaties with many countries regulating the right to fly over, take off and land there. Unless permission or new treaties with the UK are made, aviation to and from the UK may stop.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Also trains in the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Belfast%E2%80%93Dublin_line" title="Belfast\xe2\x80\x93Dublin line">Belfast\xe2\x80\x93Dublin line</a> might be stopped.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Road_Traffic" title="Vienna Convention on Road Traffic">Vienna Convention on Road Traffic</a> is written by the UN, not the EU, allowing road traffic between the UK and EU even without a deal. Ferries will continue, but with obstacles such as customs checks.<sup id="cite_ref-atclogistics_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atclogistics-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> New ferry departures between Ireland and the continent will emerge.<sup id="cite_ref-atclogistics_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atclogistics-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Options_for_continuing_relationship_with_the_EU">Options for continuing relationship with the EU</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Continuing_UK_relationship_with_the_EU" class="mw-redirect" title="Continuing UK relationship with the EU">Continuing UK relationship with the EU</a></div>\n<p>The UK\'s post-Brexit relationship with the remaining EU members could take several forms. A research paper presented to the UK Parliament in July 2013 proposed a number of alternatives to membership which would continue to allow access to the EU internal market. These include remaining in the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> negotiating deep bilateral agreements on the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland%E2%80%93European_Union_relations" title="Switzerland\xe2\x80\x93European Union relations">Swiss model</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> or exit from the EU without EEA membership or a trade agreement under the <a href="/wiki/WTO_Option" class="mw-redirect" title="WTO Option">WTO Option</a>. There may be an interim deal between the time the UK leaves the EU and when the final relationship comes in force.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Border_with_Republic_of_Ireland">Border with Republic of Ireland</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Irish_border_question" title="Irish border question">Irish border question</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg/220px-The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg/330px-The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg/440px-The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Border_on_Killeen_School_Road_-_geograph.org.uk_-_446719.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The UK/Republic of Ireland border crosses this road at Killeen (near <a href="/wiki/Newry" title="Newry">Newry</a>), marked only by a speed limit in km/h. (Northern Ireland uses mph.)</div></div></div>\n<p>There is concern about whether the border between the Republic of Ireland and Northern Ireland becomes a "hard border" with customs and passport checks on the border,<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> and whether this could affect the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> that was seen as instrumental in bringing peace to Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In order to forestall this the European Union proposed a "backstop agreement" within the Withdrawal Agreement that would put Northern Ireland under a range of EU rules in order to forestall the need for border checks.  This has been opposed by the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until March 2019, both the UK and the Republic of Ireland will be members of the EU, and therefore both are in the Customs Union and the Single Market. There is freedom of movement for all EU nationals within the <a href="/wiki/Common_Travel_Area" title="Common Travel Area">Common Travel Area</a> and there are no customs or fixed immigration controls at the border. Since 2005, the border has been essentially invisible.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Following Brexit, the border between Northern Ireland and the Republic of Ireland will become a land border between the EU and a non-EU state which may entail checks on goods at the border, depending on the co-operation and alignment of regulations between the two sides. It is therefore possible that the border will return to being a "hard" one, with fewer, controlled, crossing posts and a customs infrastructure. Both the EU and the UK have agreed this should be avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Border_with_France">Border with France</span></h3>\n<p>The President of the Regional Council of <a href="/wiki/Hauts-de-France" title="Hauts-de-France">Hauts-de-France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Bertrand" title="Xavier Bertrand">Xavier Bertrand</a>, stated in February 2016 that "If Britain leaves Europe, right away the border will leave <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a> and go to <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a>. We will not continue to guard the border for Britain if it\'s no longer in the European Union," indicating that the <a href="/wiki/Juxtaposed_controls" title="Juxtaposed controls">juxtaposed controls</a> would end with a leave vote. French Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> also suggested the agreement would be "threatened" by a leave vote.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> These claims have been disputed, as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Le_Touquet" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Le Touquet">Le Touquet 2003 treaty</a> enabling juxtaposed controls was not an EU treaty, and would not be legally void upon leaving.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Brexit vote, Xavier Bertrand asked <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois Hollande">Fran\xc3\xa7ois Hollande</a> to renegotiate the Touquet agreement,<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> which can be terminated by either party with two years\' notice.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Hollande rejected the suggestion, and said: "Calling into question the Touquet deal on the pretext that Britain has voted for Brexit and will have to start negotiations to leave the Union doesn\'t make sense." <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Cazeneuve" title="Bernard Cazeneuve">Bernard Cazeneuve</a>, the French Interior Minister, confirmed there would be "no changes to the accord". He said: "The border at Calais is closed and will remain so."<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gibraltar_and_Spain">Gibraltar and Spain</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_Brexit_on_Gibraltar" title="Effect of Brexit on Gibraltar">Effect of Brexit on Gibraltar</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier,_Winston_Churchill_Avenue,_Gibraltar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg/220px-Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg/330px-Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg/440px-Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier%2C_Winston_Churchill_Avenue%2C_Gibraltar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3288" data-file-height="2461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gibraltar_Customs_side_of_the_Spain-Gibraltar_frontier,_Winston_Churchill_Avenue,_Gibraltar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cars crossing into Gibraltar clearing customs formalities. Gibraltar is outside the customs union, VAT area and <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Schengen Zone">Schengen Zone</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Gibraltar is outside the European Union\'s common customs area and common commercial policy and so has a customs border with Spain.  Nevertheless, the territory remains within the European Union until Brexit is complete.\n</p><p>During the campaign leading up to the referendum<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Minister_of_Gibraltar" title="Chief Minister of Gibraltar">Chief Minister of Gibraltar</a> warned that Brexit posed a threat to Gibraltar\'s safety.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Gibraltar overwhelmingly voted to remain in the EU. After the result Spain\'s Foreign Minister renewed calls for joint Spanish\xe2\x80\x93British control of the peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> These calls were strongly rebuffed by Gibraltar\'s Chief Minister<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> and questions were raised over the future of free-flowing traffic at the <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar%E2%80%93Spain_border" title="Gibraltar\xe2\x80\x93Spain border">Gibraltar\xe2\x80\x93Spain border</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> The UK government states it will only negotiate on the sovereignty of Gibraltar with the consent of its people.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2018, Sir <a href="/wiki/Joe_Bossano" title="Joe Bossano">Joe Bossano</a>, Gibraltar\'s Minister for Enterprise, Training, Employment and Health and Safety (and former Chief Minister) expressed frustration at the EU\'s attitude, suggesting that Spain was being offered a veto, adding "It\'s enough to convert me from a supporter of the European Union into a Brexiteer".<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2018, Spanish Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_Dastis" title="Alfonso Dastis">Alfonso Dastis</a> announced that Spain hopes to sign off on a bilateral agreement with Britain over Gibraltar before October so as not to hinder a Brexit transition deal. Talks between London and Madrid had progressed well. While reiterating the Spanish long-term aim of "recovering" Gibraltar, he said that Spain would not hold Gibraltar as a "hostage" to the EU negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_CANZUK_countries">Relations with CANZUK countries</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/CANZUK_International" title="CANZUK International">CANZUK International</a></div>\n<p>Brexit has also created a resurgence in academic and political advocacy for negotiating trade and migration agreements with the "<a href="/wiki/CANZUK" title="CANZUK">CANZUK</a>" countries \xe2\x80\x93 those of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> This has been echoed by multiple politicians in the four countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> However, numerous academics have criticised this alternative for EU membership as "post-imperial nostalgia".<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences_of_withdrawal_for_the_EU">Consequences of withdrawal for the EU</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_Brexit_on_the_European_Union" title="Impact of Brexit on the European Union">Impact of Brexit on the European Union</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_budget">Structure and budget</span></h3>\n<p>Shortly after the referendum, the German parliament published an analysis on the consequences of a Brexit on the EU and specifically on the economic and political situation of Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> According to this, Britain is, after the United States and France, the third-most important export market for German products. In total Germany exports goods and services to Britain worth about <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">\xe2\x82\xac</a>120&#160;billion annually, which is about 8% of German exports, with Germany achieving a trade surplus with Britain worth \xe2\x82\xac36.3&#160;billion (2014). Should there be a "hard Brexit", exports would be subject to WTO customs and tariffs. The trade weighted average tariff is 2.4%, but the tariff on automobiles, for instance, is 9.7%, so trade in automobiles would be particularly affected; this would also affect German automobile manufacturers with production plants in the United Kingdom. In total, 750,000 jobs in Germany depend upon export to Britain, while on the British side about three million jobs depend on export to the EU. The study emphasises however that the predictions on the economic effects of a Brexit are subject to significant uncertainty.\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Lisbon Treaty</a> (2009), Council of the EU decisions made by <a href="/wiki/Voting_in_the_Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Voting in the Council of the European Union">qualified majority voting</a> can only be blocked if at least four members of the Council form a blocking minority. This rule was originally developed to prevent the three most populous members (Germany, France, Britain) from dominating the Council of the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> However, after a Brexit of the economically liberal British, the Germans and like-minded northern European countries (the Irish, Dutch, Scandinavians and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>) would lose an ally and therefore also their blocking minority.<sup id="cite_ref-tagesschau-nachvotum_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tagesschau-nachvotum-276">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Without this blocking minority, other EU states could overrule Germany and its allies in questions of EU budget discipline or the recruitment of German banks to guarantee deposits in troubled southern European banks.<sup id="cite_ref-welt-sperrminoritat_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welt-sperrminoritat-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With Brexit, the EU would lose its second-largest economy, the country with the third-largest population and "the financial capital of the world", as the German newspaper <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCnchner_Merkur" title="M\xc3\xbcnchner Merkur">M\xc3\xbcnchner Merkur</a> put it.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the EU would lose its second-largest net contributor to the EU budget (2015: Germany \xe2\x82\xac14.3&#160;billion, United Kingdom \xe2\x82\xac11.5&#160;billion, France \xe2\x82\xac5.5&#160;billion).<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thus, the departure of Britain would result in an additional financial burden for the remaining net contributors, unless the budget is reduced accordingly: Germany, for example, would have to pay an additional \xe2\x82\xac4.5 billion for 2019 and again for 2020; in addition, the UK would no longer be a shareholder in the <a href="/wiki/European_Investment_Bank" title="European Investment Bank">European Investment Bank</a>, in which only EU members can participate. Britain\'s share amounts to 16%, \xe2\x82\xac39.2&#160;billion (2013), which Britain would withdraw unless there is an EU treaty change.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Council_of_the_European_Union">Council of the European Union</span></h3>\n<p>The departure of the UK is expected to have a major effect on the EU.  In many policy votes Britain had allied with the relatively more economically liberal Germany who together with other northern EU allies had a blocking minority of 35% in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of the European Union</a>.  The exit of the UK from the European Union means that this blocking minority can no longer be assembled leading to speculation that it could enable the other EU countries to enforce specific proposals such as relaxing EU budget discipline or providing EU-wide deposit guarantees within the <a href="/wiki/Banking_union" title="Banking union">banking union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-welt-sperrminoritat_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welt-sperrminoritat-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament">European Parliament</span></h3>\n<p>UK MEPs are expected to retain full rights to participate in the European Parliament up to the Article 50 deadline. However, there have been discussions about excluding UK MEPs from key committee positions.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The EU will need to decide on the <a href="/wiki/Apportionment_in_the_European_Parliament" title="Apportionment in the European Parliament">revised apportionment of seats in the European Parliament</a> in time for the next European Parliament election, expected to be held in June 2019, when the United Kingdom\'s 73 MEPs will have vacated their seats. In April 2017, a group of European lawmakers discussed what should be done about the vacated seats. One plan, supported by <a href="/wiki/Gianni_Pittella" title="Gianni Pittella">Gianni Pittella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a>, is to replace the 73 seats with a pan-European constituency list; other options which were considered include dropping the British seats without replacement, and reassigning some or all of the existing seats from other countries to reduce inequality of representation.<sup id="cite_ref-politicomep_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politicomep-283">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_system">Legal system</span></h3>\n<p>The UK\'s exit from the European Union will leave Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> as the only two remaining <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> jurisdictions in the EU. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gallagher_(barrister)" title="Paul Gallagher (barrister)">Paul Gallagher</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_of_Ireland" title="Attorney General of Ireland">Attorney General of Ireland</a>, has suggested this will isolate those countries and deprive them of a powerful partner that shared a common interest in ensuring that EU legislation was not drafted or interpreted in a way that would be contrary to the principles of the common law.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lucinda_Creighton" title="Lucinda Creighton">Lucinda Creighton</a>, a former Irish government minister for legal affairs, has said that Ireland relies on the "bureaucratic capacity of the UK" to understand, influence and implement EU legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing">Fishing</span></h3>\n<p>The combined EU fishing fleets land about 6 million tonnes of fish per year,<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> of which about 3 million tonnes are from <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">UK waters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> The UK\'s share of the overall EU fishing catch is only 750,000 tonnes (830,000 tons).<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> This proportion is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_Convention" title="Fisheries Convention">London Fisheries Convention of 1964</a> and by the EU\'s <a href="/wiki/Common_Fisheries_Policy" title="Common Fisheries Policy">Common Fisheries Policy</a>. The UK government announced in July 2017 that it would end the 1964 convention in 2019. Loss of access to UK waters will particularly affect the Irish fishing industry which obtains a third of its catch there.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> The Common Fisheries Policy gives access for any member country to the waters of any other member country. The policy is generally considered a disadvantage to fish-rich countries and is a major reason why Norway and Iceland are not members.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Trade_Organization">World Trade Organization</span></h3>\n<p>Questions have arisen over how existing international arrangements with the EU under <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> (WTO) terms should evolve.  Some countries&#160;&#8211;&#32;such as Australia and the United States&#160;&#8211;&#32;wish to challenge the basis for division (i.e., division between the UK and the continuing EU) of the trade schedules previously agreed between them and the EU, because it reduces their flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Brexit&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the WTO does not have any protocols covering trade in services.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion_and_comment">Public opinion and comment</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_comment_up_to_February_2017_UK_white_paper">Public comment up to February 2017 UK white paper</span></h3>\n<p>Various EU leaders said that they would not start any negotiation before the UK formally invokes Article 50. <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Juncker" title="Jean-Claude Juncker">Jean-Claude Juncker</a> ordered all members of the EU Commission not to engage in any kind of contact with UK parties regarding Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> In October 2016, he stated that he was agitated that the British had not developed a sense of community with Europeans during 40 years of membership; Juncker denied that Brexit was a warning for the EU, envisaged developing an EU defence policy without the British after Brexit, and rejected a suggestion that the EU should negotiate in such a way that Britain would be able to hold a second referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-293">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> On 5 November 2016, Juncker reacted to reports of some European businesses seeking to make agreements with the UK government, and warned: "I am telling them [companies] that they should not interfere in the debate, as they will find that I will block their path."<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Juncker stated in February 2017 that the UK would be expected to pay outstanding commitments to EU projects and pensions as part of the withdrawal process, suggesting such bills would be "very hefty."<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>German foreign secretary <a href="/wiki/Frank-Walter_Steinmeier" title="Frank-Walter Steinmeier">Frank-Walter Steinmeier</a> met Britain\'s foreign secretary <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> on 4 November 2016; Johnson stressed the importance of British-German relationships, whereas Steinmeier responded that the German view was that the UK should have voted to stay in the EU and that the German priority now was to preserve the remaining union of 27 members. There could be no negotiations before the UK formally gives notice. A long delay before beginning negotiations would be detrimental. Britain could not keep the advantages of the single market but at the same time cancel the "less pleasant rules".<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newly appointed prime minister Theresa May made clear that negotiations with the EU required a "UK-wide approach". On 15 July 2016, she said: "I have already said that I won\'t be triggering article 50 until I think that we have a UK approach and objectives for negotiations \xe2\x80\x93 I think it is important that we establish that before we trigger article 50."<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <i>The Daily Telegraph</i>, the Department for Exiting the European Union spent over \xc2\xa3250,000 on legal advice from top Government lawyers in two months, and had plans to recruit more people. <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> said the figures showed the Civil Service was unprepared for the very complex negotiations ahead.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the United Kingdom\'s vote to <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">leave the European Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_International_Trade" title="Department for International Trade">Department for International Trade</a> (DIT) for striking and extending trade agreements between the UK and non-EU states was created by <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, shortly after she took office on 13 July 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-csw_new_article_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csw_new_article-299">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> It employs about 200 trade negotiators<sup id="cite_ref-economist2017_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist2017-300">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> and is overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_International_Trade" title="Secretary of State for International Trade">Secretary of State for International Trade</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Liam_Fox" title="Liam Fox">Liam Fox</a>.\n</p><p>On 17 January 2017, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, announced a series of 12 negotiating objectives in a speech at <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House" title="Lancaster House">Lancaster House</a>. These consist of an end to <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> jurisdiction, withdrawal from the <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">single market</a> with a "comprehensive free-trade agreement" replacing this, a new customs agreement excluding the <a href="/wiki/Common_external_tariff" title="Common external tariff">common external tariff</a> and the <a href="/wiki/EU%27s_common_commercial_policy" class="mw-redirect" title="EU&#39;s common commercial policy">EU\'s common commercial policy</a>, an end to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">free movement of people</a>, co-operation in crime and terrorism, collaboration in areas of science and technology, engagement with devolved administrations, maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Common_Travel_Area" title="Common Travel Area">Common Travel Area</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, and preserving existing workers\' rights. She also confirmed, "that the Government will put the final deal that is agreed between the UK and the EU to a [<a href="/wiki/Meaningful_vote" title="Meaningful vote">meaningful] vote</a> in both Houses of Parliament, before it comes into force."<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Government has stated its intention to "secure the specific interests of Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland, as well as those of all parts of England". Through the Joint Ministerial Committee on EU Negotiations (JMC(EN)), the Government intends to involve the views of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> in the process of negotiating the UK\'s exit from the EU. For instance, at the January 2017 meeting of the JMC(EN), the Scottish Government\'s proposal to remain in the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> was considered.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_comment_pre-_and_post-Article_50_notification">Public comment pre- and post-Article 50 notification</span></h3>\n<p>EU negotiator <a href="/wiki/Guy_Verhofstadt" title="Guy Verhofstadt">Guy Verhofstadt</a>, the European parliament\'s chief negotiator, said that: "All British citizens today have also EU citizenship. That means a number of things: the possibility to participate in the European elections, the freedom of travel without problem inside the union. We need to have an arrangement in which this arrangement can continue for those citizens who on an individual basis are requesting it." The suggestion being an \xe2\x80\x9cassociate citizenship\xe2\x80\x9d.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An EU meeting to discuss Brexit was called for 29 April 2017, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Tusk" title="Donald Tusk">Donald Tusk</a> stating that the "priority would be giving "clarity" to EU residents, business and member states about the talks ahead". Barnier called for talks to be completed by October 2018 to give time for any agreement to be ratified before the UK leaves in March 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> called for a referendum to create a united Ireland, following the Northern Ireland majority decision (56% to 44%) to vote no to Brexit and 2 March election to the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> wherein Sinn F\xc3\xa9in increased its number of seats.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early May, Jean-Claude Juncker said that the UK leaving the EU was a "tragedy" and that it is partly the responsibility of the EU. "The EU, in many respects has done too much, especially the Commission", including "too much regulation and too many interferences in the lives of our fellow citizens". The European Commission has, following the "Better regulation" initiative, in place since before Brexit, reduced the number of legislative proposals from 130 to 23 per year.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-referendum_opinion_polling">Post-referendum opinion polling</span></h3>\n<h4><span id="Right_.2F_Wrong"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_/_Wrong">Right / Wrong</span></h4>\n<p>Following the EU referendum, there have been many opinion polls on the question of whether <b>the UK was "right" or "wrong" to vote to leave the EU</b>. does not explicitly ask how respondents would vote in a referendum.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Three-option_referendum">Three-option referendum</span></h4>\n<p>On 6 July 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">UK Cabinet</a> agreed a statement at <a href="/wiki/Chequers" title="Chequers">Chequers</a> that set out a proposal for <a href="/wiki/The_future_relationship_between_the_United_Kingdom_and_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="The future relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union">the future relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> following which two members of the Cabinet resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> On 16 July 2018 the former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Education" title="Secretary of State for Education">Education Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Justine_Greening" title="Justine Greening">Justine Greening</a> noted the lack of a political consensus behind the Chequers proposal and said that, due to a \'stalemate\' in the House of Commons, the issue of Brexit should be referred back to the electorate.  She proposed a referendum with three options: to leave the EU on such terms as might be agreed between the UK Government and the EU 27; to leave the EU without agreed terms; or to remain in the EU.  Voters would be asked to mark a first and second preference using the <a href="/wiki/Supplementary_vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Supplementary vote">supplementary vote</a> system.  If there were no majority for any particular option among first-preference votes, the third-placed option would be eliminated and second preferences would be used to determine the winner from the two remaining options.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following table shows opinion polls that have been conducted on how people would vote in such a <b>three-option referendum</b>.  The table shows the poll results for a first round in which all three options would be available, and for a second round in which only the top two options in the first round would be available.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable mw-collapsible" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;line-height:14px">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Date(s) conducted\n</th>\n<th data-sort-=""
