class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Brexit/Post-referendum_opinion_polling:_Remain-Leave" title="Template talk:Brexit/Post-referendum opinion polling: Remain-Leave"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Brexit/Post-referendum_opinion_polling:_Remain-Leave&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-DealOrNoDeal-334"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-DealOrNoDeal_334-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Referendum on whether to accept the negotiated terms or leave the EU without a deal.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-AcceptOrRemain-335"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AcceptOrRemain_335-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Referendum on whether to accept the negotiated terms or remain in the EU.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-SecondEUReferendum-349"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-SecondEUReferendum_349-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Question asked about a second EU referendum, not necessarily on the final deal.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<p><br />\n</p><p>In July 2017, LSE/Opinium research indicated that 60% of <a href="/wiki/Briton" class="mw-redirect" title="Briton">Britons</a> wanted to retain <a href="/wiki/Citizenship_of_the_European_Union" title="Citizenship of the European Union">EU citizenship</a> after Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2018 polls by <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> and <a href="/wiki/ICM_Research" title="ICM Research">ICM Research</a> both suggest <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> could make substantial gains by supporting a second referendum on Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_position">US position</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Rex_Tillerson" title="Rex Tillerson">Rex Tillerson</a>, former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a>, considers that the European Union and the United Kingdom should perform a fast Brexit to avoid useless disagreement.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_responses_to_the_referendum_vote">Cultural responses to the referendum vote</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For main article on Brexit in Art and Literature, see <a href="/wiki/Brexit_in_Literature_and_the_Arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Brexit in Literature and the Arts">Brexit in Literature and the Arts</a>.</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit_in_the_visual_arts">Brexit in the visual arts</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg/220px-Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg/330px-Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg/440px-Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1113" data-file-height="704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brexit-is-a-monstrosity-float-2017-10-01-in-manchester-photo-robert-mandel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-Brexit protesters in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DBG_22473_(26370830218).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg/220px-DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg/330px-DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg/440px-DBG_22473_%2826370830218%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4800" data-file-height="3200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DBG_22473_(26370830218).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="D\xc3\xbcsseldorf">D\xc3\xbcsseldorf</a> carnival parade in February 2018</div></div></div>\n<p>The response of artists and writers to Brexit has in general been negative, reflecting a reported overwhelming percentage of people involved in Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Creative_industries" title="Creative industries">creative industries</a> voting against leaving the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Responses by visual artists to Brexit include a mural, painted in May 2017, by the secretive graffiti artist <a href="/wiki/Banksy" title="Banksy">Banksy</a> near the ferry port at <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. It shows a workman using a chisel to chip off one of the stars on the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union Flag">European Union Flag</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his 2017 art exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_Gallery" class="mw-redirect" title="Serpentine Gallery">Serpentine Gallery</a> in London, the artist <a href="/wiki/Grayson_Perry" title="Grayson Perry">Grayson Perry</a> showed a series of ceramic, tapestry and other works of art dealing with the divisions in Britain during the Brexit campaign and in its aftermath. This included two large ceramic pots, Perry called his <i>Brexit Vases,</i> standing on plinths ten feet apart, on the first of which were scenes involving pro-European British citizens, and on the second scenes involving anti-European British citizens. These were derived from what Perry called his "Brexit tour of Britain."<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit_in_novels">Brexit in novels</span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Daphne_Du_Maurier" class="mw-redirect" title="Daphne Du Maurier">Daphne Du Maurier</a>\'s 1972 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Rule_Britannia_(novel)" title="Rule Britannia (novel)">Rule Britannia</a></i> the UK is brought to the brink of bankruptcy after withdrawal from the <a href="/wiki/EEC" class="mw-redirect" title="EEC">EEC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the first novels to engage with a post-Brexit Britain was <i>Rabbitman</i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Paraskos" title="Michael Paraskos">Michael Paraskos</a> (published 9 March 2017). <i>Rabbitman</i> is a dark comic fantasy in which the events that lead to the election of a right-wing populist American president, who happens also to be a rabbit, and Britain\'s vote to leave the European Union, were the result of a series of <a href="/wiki/Faustian" class="mw-redirect" title="Faustian">Faustian</a> pacts with the <a href="/wiki/Devil" title="Devil">Devil</a>. As a result, <i>Rabbitman</i> is set partly in a post-Brexit Britain in which society has collapsed and people are dependent on European Union food aid.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mark Billingham\xe2\x80\x99s <i>Love Like Blood</i> (published 1 June 2017) is a crime thriller in which Brexit sees a rise in xenophobic hate crime.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> In the novel <i>The Remains of the Way</i> (published 6 June 2017), <a href="/wiki/David_Boyle_(author)" title="David Boyle (author)">David Boyle</a> imagines Brexit was a conspiracy led by a forgotten government <a href="/wiki/Quango" title="Quango">quango</a>, still working away in <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a>, originally set up by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a> in the sixteenth century during the reign of <a href="/wiki/King_Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="King Henry VIII">King Henry VIII</a>, and now dedicated to a Protestant Brexit.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Post-Brexit Britain is also the setting for <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Craig" title="Amanda Craig">Amanda Craig</a>\'s <i>The Lie of the Land</i> (published 13 June 2017), a satirical novel set ten years after the vote to leave the European Union, in which an impoverished <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> couple from <a href="/wiki/Islington" title="Islington">Islington</a> in north London are forced to move from the heart of the pro-European Union capital, to the heart of the pro-Brexit countryside in Devon.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Brexit is also the baseline for Douglas Board\xe2\x80\x99s comic political thriller <i>Time of Lies</i> (published 23 June 2017). In this novel, the first post-Brexit <a href="/wiki/General_election" title="General election">general election</a> in 2020 is won by a violent right-wing former football hooligan called Bob Grant. Board charts the response to this of the hitherto pro-European Union metropolitan political elite.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stanley_Johnson_(writer)" title="Stanley Johnson (writer)">Stanley Johnson</a>\'s <i>Kompromat</i> (scheduled for July 2017) is a political thriller that suggests the vote to leave the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> was a result of Russian influence on the referendum, although Johnson has insisted his book is not intended to point the finger at Russia\'s <a href="/wiki/Secret_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Secret services">secret services</a>, but is "just meant to be fun."<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit_in_theatre">Brexit in theatre</span></h3>\n<p>In June 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_National_Theatre" title="Royal National Theatre">National Theatre</a> in London presented a play by <a href="/wiki/Carol_Ann_Duffy" title="Carol Ann Duffy">Carol Ann Duffy</a>, entitled <i>My Country; a work in progress</i>. An allegorical work, the play uses the device of a convention called by the goddess <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a>, who is concerned about the future of the British people.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> The play differs from some artistic responses in that Duffy and the National Theatre-based the attitudes of the characters in part on the responses of ordinary people in interviews that were conducted by the regional offices of the UK Arts Councils, but excluding responses from London and the south-east of England, where most people voted not to leave the EU. As a result, according to Dominic Cavendish, writing in <i>The Daily Telegraph</i>, "the bias is towards the Leave camp".<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit_in_film">Brexit in film</span></h3>\n<p>In 2016, the television director <a href="/wiki/Martin_Durkin_(television_director)" title="Martin Durkin (television director)">Martin Durkin</a> wrote and directed an 81 minute long <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/Brexit:_The_Movie" title="Brexit: The Movie">Brexit: The Movie</a></i>, which advocated with the withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. The film was produced by the production company Wag TV with a budget of \xc2\xa3300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> The production costs were sourced primarily through crowdfunding via Kickstarter alongside a \xc2\xa350,000 contribution from the hedge fund Spitfire Capital. In May 2016 the film premiered in Leicester Square, with notable figures such as <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Davis_(British_politician)" title="David Davis (British politician)">David Davis</a> (who later became <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" title="Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union">Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union</a>) in attendance. \n</p><p>A documentary film was released in 2018, called <i><a href="/wiki/Postcards_from_the_48%25" title="Postcards from the 48%">Postcards from the 48%</a></i>, which is described on the film\'s website as: "A documentary film made by and featuring those who voted Remain, the 48%, to show the other 27 EU Member States that it was far from a landslide victory and just why we are fighting to stay part of the EU."<sup id="cite_ref-Postcards_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Postcards-366">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> A review on Shadows on the Wall ("the UK\'s first film <a href="/wiki/Ezine" class="mw-redirect" title="Ezine">ezine</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup>) wrote: \xe2\x80\xb3This is a comprehensive, factual exploration of the issue, grappling with the referendum, its ramifications and the way the split vote has fractured British society.\xe2\x80\xb3<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_pro-European_political_organisations">Establishment of pro-European political organisations</span></h2>\n<p>Following the Brexit vote, there have been several attempts to set up a new <a href="/wiki/Pro-Europeanism" title="Pro-Europeanism">pro-European</a> political party.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-buzzfeed.com_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buzzfeed.com-370">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Examples include \'The Democrats\' (a proposal by former <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> political editor <a href="/wiki/James_Chapman_(journalist)" title="James Chapman (journalist)">James Chapman</a>), \'The Radicals\' (proposed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jeremy_Cliffe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jeremy Cliffe (page does not exist)">Jeremy Cliffe</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> bureau chief of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Renew_Britain" title="Renew Britain">Renew Britain</a> party.<sup id="cite_ref-buzzfeed.com_370-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buzzfeed.com-370">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, newly elected <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Vince_Cable" title="Vince Cable">Vince Cable</a> criticised \'pop up\' anti-Brexit parties formed following the 2016 referendum, saying of those groups\' policies  "...it is the kind of ideology-free, technocratic, authoritarian centrism that would be more at home in, say, Singapore." and "Voters beware."<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small" class="reference-text">European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018, section 20.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/uk-governments-preparations-for-a-no-deal-scenario/uk-governments-preparations-for-a-no-deal-scenario">"UK government\'s preparations for a \'no deal\' scenario"</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">31 May</span> 2017</span>. <span class="cs1-subscription">(Subscription required (<span title="The site requires a paid subscription to access this page.">help</span>))</span>. <q>Through analysis of the EU treaty database, the FT found 759 separate EU bilateral agreements with potential relevance to Britain, covering trade in nuclear goods, customs, fisheries, trade, transport and regulatory co-operation in areas such as antitrust or financial services. This includes multilateral agreements based on consensus, where Britain must re-approach 132 separate parties. Around 110 separate opt-in accords at the UN and World Trade Organisation are excluded from the estimates, as are narrow agreements on the environment, health, research and science. Some additional UK bilateral deals, outside the EU framework, may also need to be revised because they make reference to EU law. Some of the 759 are so essential that it would be unthinkable to operate without them. Air services agreements allow British aeroplanes to land in America, Canada or Israel; nuclear accords permit the trade in spare parts and fuel for Britain\'s power stations. Both these sectors are excluded from trade negotiations and must be addressed separately.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Financial+Times&amp;rft.atitle=After+Brexit%3A+the+UK+will+need+to+renegotiate+at+least+759+treaties&amp;rft.date=2017-05-30&amp;rft.aulast=McClean&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.ft.com%2Fcontent%2Ff1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABrexit" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-246"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-246">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Gerrard, Bradley (18 August 2017). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.telegraph.co.uk/business/2017/08/18/no-plan-b-brexit-stakes-aviation-sector-sky-high/">"With no plan B, Brexit stakes for aviation sector are sky high"</a>. <i>The Daily Telegraph</i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
